12372-8{_}1288{_}At the same time, the bodies of Valerian, Tiburtius, and Maximus were found in a neighboring cemetery, and, together with the relics of Pope Urban,--as well as the body of St. Cecilia,--were placed under the high altar of her church.
12372-8{_}1289{_}[H] The cypress coffin in which she had been reverently laid at the time of her death was preserved and set within a marble sarcophagus.
12372-8{_}1290{_}No expense was spared by the devout Paschal to adorn the church that had been so signally favored.
12372-8{_}1291{_}All the Art of the time (and at that time the arts flourished only in the service of the Church) was called upon to assist in making the new basilica magnificent.
12372-8{_}1292{_}The mosaics which were set up to adorn the apse and the arch of triumph were among the best works of the century, and, with colors still brilliant and design still unimpaired, they hold their place at the present day, and carry back the thought and the imagination of the beholder a thousand years into the very heart of this old story.
12372-8{_}1293{_}Under the great mosaic of the apse one may still read the inscription, in the rude Latin of the century, which tells of Paschal's zeal and Rome's joy, closing with the line,    "Roma resultat ovans semper ornata per   aevum."
12372-8{_}1294{_}[Footnote H: It is a remarkable fact, to be explained by the believers in the virtue of relics, that, notwithstanding the body of St. Cecilia was deposited perfect in her grave, and, as we shall see, was long after found complete, no less than five heads of St. Cecilia are declared to exist, or to have existed,--for one has been lost,--in different churches.
12372-8{_}1295{_}One is in the church of the SS.
12372-8{_}1296{_}Quattro Coronati, at Rome, which possessed it from a very early period; a second is at Paris, a third at Beauvais, a fourth was at Tours, and we have seen the reliquary in which a fifth is preserved in the old cathedral of Torcello.]
12372-8{_}1297{_}And thus once more the body of the virgin was left to repose in peace, once more the devout could offer their prayers to the Saint at the altar consecrated by her presence, and once more the superstitious could increase the number of the miracles wrought by her favor.
12372-8{_}1298{_}Through the long period of the fall and depression of Rome, her church continued to be a favorite one with the people of the city, and with the pilgrims to it.
12372-8{_}1299{_}From time to time it was repaired and adorned, and in the thirteenth century the walls of its portico were covered with a series of frescoes, representing the events of St. Cecilia's life, and the finding of her body by Paschal.
12372-8{_}1300{_}These frescoes--precious as specimens of reawakening Art, and especially precious at Rome, because of the little that was done there at that period--were all, save one, long since destroyed in some "restoration" of the church.
12372-8{_}1301{_}The one that was preserved is now within the church, and represents in its two divisions the burial of the Saint by Pope Urban, and her appearance in St. Peter's Church to the sleeping Paschal, whose figure is rendered with amusing naïveté and literalness.
12372-8{_}1302{_}Meanwhile, after the translation of St. Cecilia's body, the catacombs remained much in the same neglected state as before, falling more and more into ruin, but still visited from year to year by the pilgrims, whom even pillage and danger could not keep from Rome.
12372-8{_}1303{_}For two centuries,--from the thirteenth to the fifteenth,--scarcely any mention of them is to be found.
12372-8{_}1304{_}Petrarch, in his many letters about Rome, dwells often on the sacredness of the soil within the city, in whose crypts and churches so many saints and martyrs lie buried, but hardly refers to the catacombs themselves, and never in such a way as to show that they were an object of interest to him, though a lover of all Roman relics and a faithful worshipper of the saints.
12372-8{_}1305{_}It was near the end of the sixteenth century that a happy accident--the falling in of the road outside the Porta Salara--brought to light the streets of the Cemetery of St. Priscilla, and awakened in Antonio Bosio a zeal for the exploration of the catacombs which led him to devote the remainder of his long life to the pursuit, and by study, investigation, and observation, to lay the solid basis of the thorough and comprehensive acquaintance with subterranean Rome which has been extended by the researches of a long line of able scholars down to the present day.
12372-8{_}1306{_}But to Bosio the chief honor is due, as the earliest, the most exact, and the most indefatigable of the explorers.
12372-8{_}1307{_}It was during his lifetime that the story of St. Cecilia received a continuation, of which he himself has left us a full account.
12372-8{_}1308{_}In the year 1599, Paolo Emilio Sfondrati, Cardinal of the Title of St. Cecilia,[I] undertook a thorough restoration of the old basilica erected by Paschal.
12372-8{_}1309{_}He possessed a large collection of relics, and determined that he would place the most precious of them under the high altar.
12372-8{_}1310{_}For this purpose the vault containing the sarcophagi in which St. Cecilia and her companions lay must be opened, and on the 20th of October the work was undertaken.
12372-8{_}1311{_}Upon breaking through the wall, two sarcophagi of white marble were discovered.
12372-8{_}1312{_}The Cardinal was on the spot, and, in the presence of numerous dignitaries of the Church, whom he had sent for as witnesses, he caused the heavy top of the first of these stone coffins to be lifted.
12372-8{_}1313{_}Within was seen the chest of cypress-wood in which, according to the old story, the Saint had been originally placed.
12372-8{_}1314{_}Sfondrati with his own hands removed the lid, and within the chest was found the body of the virgin, with a silken veil spread over her rich dress, on which could still be seen the stains of blood, while at her feet yet lay the bloody cloths which had been placed there more than thirteen centuries before.
12372-8{_}1315{_}She was lying upon her right side, her feet a little drawn up, her arms extended and resting one upon the other, her neck turned so that her head rested upon the left cheek.
12372-8{_}1316{_}Her form perfectly preserved, and her attitude of the sweetest virginal grace and modesty, it seemed as if she lay there asleep rather than dead.
12372-8{_}1317{_}[J]--The second sarcophagus was found to contain three bodies, which were recognized as being, according to tradition, those of Tiburtius, Valerian, and Maximus.
12372-8{_}1318{_}[Footnote I: The _Titoli_ of Rome correspond nearly to Parishes.
12372-8{_}1319{_}They date from an early period in the history of the Church.]
12372-8{_}1320{_}[Footnote J: "Dormientis instar," says Bosio, in his _Relatio Inventionis et Repositionis S. Caeciliae et Sociorum_.
12372-8{_}1321{_}The discovery of the body of the Saint in this perfect state of preservation has, of course, been attributed by many Romanist authors to miraculous interposition.
12372-8{_}1322{_}But it is to be accounted for by natural causes.
12372-8{_}1323{_}The soil of the catacombs and of Rome is in many parts remarkable for its antiseptic qualities.
12372-8{_}1324{_}The Cavaliere de Rossi informed us that he had been present at the opening of an ancient tomb on the Appian Way, in which the body of a young man had been found in a state of entire preservation, fresh almost as on the day of its burial, and with it was a piece of sponge which had apparently been soaked in blood,--for his death had been by violence.
12372-8{_}1325{_}In the winter of 1857, two marble sarcophagi were found in one of the passages of the Catacombs of St. Callixtus, in which excavations were then going on, and upon being opened, a body was found in each, in a state, not of entire, but of almost perfect preservation.
12372-8{_}1326{_}The skin had become somewhat shrunk, and the flesh was hardened and darkened, but the general form and features were preserved.
12372-8{_}1327{_}Possibly these also may have been the bodies of saints.
12372-8{_}1328{_}The sarcophagi were kept through the winter in the catacombs where they were found, and their marble lids being removed, covers of glass were fitted to them, so that the bodies might be seen by the visitors to the catacombs.
12372-8{_}1329{_}It was a frequent custom, chiefly in the fourth and fifth centuries, to bury the rich in sarcophagi placed within tombs in the catacombs.]
12372-8{_}1330{_}The day advanced as these discoveries were made, and Sfondrati having had a chest of wood hastily lined with silk, and brought to a room in the adjoining convent, which opened into the church, (it is the room at the left, now used for the first reception of novices,) carried the cypress chest with its precious contents to this apartment, and placed it within the new box, which he locked and sealed.
12372-8{_}1331{_}Then, taking the key with him, he hastened to go out to Frascati, where Pope Clement VIII.
12372-8{_}1332{_}was then staying, to avoid the early autumn airs of Rome.
12372-8{_}1333{_}The Pope was in bed with the gout, and gave audience to no one; but when he heard of the great news that Sfondrati had brought, he desired at once to see him, and to hear from him the account of the discovery.
12372-8{_}1334{_}"The Pope groaned and grieved that he was not well enough to hasten at once to visit and salute so great a martyr."
12372-8{_}1335{_}But it happened that the famous annalist, Cardinal Baronius, was then with the Pope at Frascati, and Clement ordered him to go to Rome forthwith, in his stead, to behold and venerate the body of the Saint.
12372-8{_}1336{_}Sfondrati immediately took Baronius in his carriage back to the city, and in the evening they reached the Church of St.
12372-8{_}1337{_}Cecilia.
12372-8{_}1338{_}[K] Baronius, in the account which he has left of these transactions, expresses in simple words his astonishment and delight at seeing the preservation of the cypress chest, and of the body of the Saint: "When we at length beheld the sacred body, it was then, that, according to the words of David, 'as we had heard, so we saw, in the city of the Lord of Hosts, in the city of our God.
12372-8{_}1339{_}'[L] For as we had read that the venerated body of Cecilia had been found and laid away by Paschal the Pope, so we found it."
12372-8{_}1340{_}He describes at length the posture of the virgin, who lay like one sleeping, in such modest and noble attitude, that "whoever beheld her was struck with unspeakable reverence, as if the heavenly Spouse stood by as a guard watching his sleeping Bride, warning and threatening: 'Awake not my love till she please.
12372-8{_}1341{_}'"[M] The next morning, Baronius performed Mass in the church in memory and honor of St. Cecilia, and the other saints buried near her, and then returned to Frascati to report to the Pope what he had seen.
12372-8{_}1342{_}It was resolved to push forward the works on the church with vigor, and to replace the body of the Saint under its altar on her feast-day, the twenty-second of November, with the most solemn pontifical ceremony.
12372-8{_}1343{_}[Footnote K: This account is to be found in the _Annals_ of Baronius, _ad annum_ 821.]
12372-8{_}1344{_}[Footnote L: Psalm xlviii.
12372-8{_}1345{_}8.]
12372-8{_}1346{_}[Footnote M: Song of Solomon, ii.
12372-8{_}1347{_}7.]
12372-8{_}1348{_}Meanwhile the report of the wonderful discovery spread through Rome, and caused general excitement and emotion.
12372-8{_}1349{_}The Trasteverini, with whom Cecilia had always been a favorite saint, were filled with joy, with piety, and superstition.
12372-8{_}1350{_}Crowds continually pressed to the church, and so great was the ardor of worshippers, that the Swiss guards of the court were needed to preserve order.
12372-8{_}1351{_}Lamps were kept constantly burning around the coffin, which was set near a grating in the wall between the church and convent, so as to be visible to the devout.
12372-8{_}1352{_}"There was no need of burning perfumes and incense near the sacred body, for a sweetest odor breathed out from it, like that of roses and lilies."
12372-8{_}1353{_}Sfondrati, desirous to preserve for future generations a memorial likeness of the Saint, ordered the sculptor Stefano Maderno to make a statue which should represent the body of Cecilia as it was found lying in the cypress chest.
12372-8{_}1354{_}Maderno was then a youth of twenty-three years.
12372-8{_}1355{_}Sculpture at this time in Rome had fallen into a miserable condition of degraded conventionalism and extravagance.
12372-8{_}1356{_}But Maderno was touched with the contagion of the religious enthusiasm of the moment, and his work is full of simple dignity, noble grace, and tender beauty.
12372-8{_}1357{_}No other work of the time is to be compared with it.
12372-8{_}1358{_}It is a memorial not only of the loveliness of the Saint, but of the self-forgetful religious fervor of the artist, at a period when every divine impulse seemed to be absent from the common productions of Art.
12372-8{_}1359{_}Rome has no other statue of such sacred charm, none more inspired with Christian feeling.
12372-8{_}1360{_}It lies in front of the high altar, disfigured by a silver crown and a costly necklace, the offerings of vulgar and pretentious adoration; but even thus it is at once a proof and prophecy of what Art is to accomplish under the influence of the Christian spirit.
12372-8{_}1361{_}The inscription that Sfondrati placed before the statue still exists.
12372-8{_}1362{_}It is as follows: "Behold the image of the most holy virgin Cecilia; whom I, Paul, Cardinal of the Title of St. Cecilia, saw lying perfect in her sepulchre; which I have caused to be made in this marble, in the very position of the body, for you."
12372-8{_}1363{_}The twenty-second of November arrived.
12372-8{_}1364{_}The Pope had recovered from his gout.
12372-8{_}1365{_}The church was splendidly decorated.
12372-8{_}1366{_}A solemn procession, illustrated by the presence of all the great dignitaries of the Church, of the ambassadors of foreign states, and the nobles of Rome, advanced up the nave.
12372-8{_}1367{_}Clement intoned the Mass.
12372-8{_}1368{_}Then proceeding to the cypress chest, it was lifted by four cardinals, and carried to the vault under the altar, while the choir chanted the anthem, _O beata Coecilia, quoe Almachium superâsti, Tiburtium et Valerianum ad martyrii coronam vocâsti!_ The old coffin, undisturbed, was placed in a silver case; the last service was performed, and the body of the virgin was once more laid away to rest.
12372-8{_}1369{_}We pass now over two centuries and a half.
12372-8{_}1370{_}About five years ago the Cavaliere de Rossi found lying upon the ground, in a _vigna_ bordering on the Appian Way, about two miles from Rome, a portion of a sepulchral stone on which were the letters NELIUS MARTYR, the NE broken across.
12372-8{_}1371{_}He immediately conjectured that this was a piece of the stone that had covered the grave of Pope Cornelius, [A.D. 250-252,] and on the truth of this conjecture important results depended.
12372-8{_}1372{_}It was known that this pope had been buried in the Catacombs of St. Callixtus; and it was known also, from the itineraries and some other sources, that his grave was not in the same chamber with the graves of the other popes who were buried in those catacombs, but that it was not far away from it.
12372-8{_}1373{_}It was further known, as we have seen, that the chapel in which St. Cecilia had been buried was close to the Chamber of the Popes.
12372-8{_}1374{_}But a tradition dating from a late period of the Middle Ages had given the name of Callixtus to the catacombs opening from the Church of St. Sebastian, at a little greater distance from Rome.
12372-8{_}1375{_}In these catacombs the place supposed to be that of St. Cecilia's grave was pointed out, and an inscription set up to mark the spot, by a French archbishop, in the year 1409, still exists.
12372-8{_}1376{_}Many indications, however, led De Rossi to disbelieve this tradition and to distrust this authority.
12372-8{_}1377{_}It contradicted the brief indications of the itineraries, and could not be reconciled with other established facts.
12372-8{_}1378{_}Not far from the place where the broken inscription was found was an accidental entrance into catacombs which had been supposed to have been originally connected with those of St. Sebastian, but were believed by De Rossi to be a portion of the veritable Catacombs of St. Callixtus, and quite separate from the former.
12372-8{_}1379{_}The paths in this part, however, were stopped up in so many directions, that it was impossible to get an entrance through them to such parts as might determine the question.
12372-8{_}1380{_}Again, in the neighborhood of the discovery of the broken stone was an old building, used as a stable, and for other mean purposes.
12372-8{_}1381{_}On examination of it, De Rossi satisfied himself that it had been originally one of the churches erected in the fourth century at the entrance of the catacombs, and he had little doubt that he had now found the place of the main descent into the Catacombs of St. Callixtus.
12372-8{_}1382{_}The discovery was a great one; for near the main entrance had been the burial-place of the popes, and of St. Cecilia.
12372-8{_}1383{_}De Rossi laid the results of his inductive process of archaeological reasoning before the pope, who immediately gave orders for the purchase of the _vigna_, and directions that excavations should be at once begun.
12372-8{_}1384{_}[N]  [Footnote N: Another curious point was made by De Rossi previously to the commencement of the explorations.
12372-8{_}1385{_}It illustrates the accuracy of his acquaintance with the underground archaeology.
12372-8{_}1386{_}In one of the itineraries it was said, speaking of the burial-place of Cornelius, that here also St. Cyprian was buried.
12372-8{_}1387{_}Now, as is well known, Cyprian was buried in Africa, where he had suffered martyrdom.
12372-8{_}1388{_}His martyrdom took place on the same day with that of Cornelius, though in another year; and their memories were consequently celebrated by the Church on the same day, the 16th of September.
12372-8{_}1389{_}De Rossi declared, that, if he discovered the tomb of St. Cornelius, he should find near it something which would explain the error of the itinerary in stating that Cyprian's grave also was here.
12372-8{_}1390{_}And such proved to be the fact.
12372-8{_}1391{_}On the wall, by the side of the grave, was found a painting of Cornelius, with his name, "S[=c][=s] Cornelius," and by the side of this figure was another painting of a bishop in his robes, with the letters "S[=c][=s] Ciprianus."]
12372-8{_}1392{_}[Transcriber's note: Here and below the = sign is used to indicate an overscore.]
12372-8{_}1393{_}The work was scarcely begun, before an ancient stairway, long ago buried under accumulated earth and rubbish, was discovered, leading down to the second story of the catacombs.
12372-8{_}1394{_}The passages into which it opened were filled with earth, but, as this was cleared away, a series of chambers of unusual size, reaching almost to the surface of the soil, was entered upon.
12372-8{_}1395{_}At the right a wide door led into a large chapel.
12372-8{_}1396{_}The walls were covered with rudely scratched names and inscriptions, some in Greek and some in Latin.
12372-8{_}1397{_}De Rossi, whose eyes were practised in the work, undertook to decipher these often obscure scribblings.
12372-8{_}1398{_}They were for the most part the inscriptions of the pilgrims who had visited these places, and their great number gave proof that this was a most important portion of the cemetery.
12372-8{_}1399{_}The majority of these were simply names, or names accompanied with short expressions of piety.
12372-8{_}1400{_}Many, for instance, were in such form as this,--[Greek: Elaphin eis mneian echete],--"Keep Elaphis in remembrance."
12372-8{_}1401{_}Many were expressions of devotion, written by the pilgrims for the sake of those who were dear to them, as,--_Vivat in Domino_, "May he live in the Lord"; _Pet[ite] ut Verecundus cum suis bene naviget_, "Seek that Verecundus with his companions may voyage prosperously."
12372-8{_}1402{_}The character of the writing, the names and the style, indicate that these inscriptions belong mostly to the third and fourth centuries.
12372-8{_}1403{_}Among these writings on the wall were one or two which confirmed De Rossi in the opinion that this must be the sepulchre in which the greater number of the popes of the third century had been buried.
12372-8{_}1404{_}Carefully preserving all the mass of rubbish which was taken from the chamber, he set himself to its examination, picking out from it all the bits and fragments of marble, upon many of which letters or portions of letters were cut.
12372-8{_}1405{_}Most of them were of that elaborate character which is well known to all readers of the inscriptions from the catacombs as that of Pope Damasus,--for this Pope [A.D. 366-385] had devoted himself to putting up new inscriptions over celebrated graves, and had used a peculiar and sharply cut letter, easy to be distinguished.
12372-8{_}1406{_}It was known that he had put new inscriptions over the tombs of the popes buried in the Cemetery of St. Callixtus.
12372-8{_}1407{_}After most patient examination, De Rossi succeeded in finding and putting together the inscriptions of four of these early popes, and, with Cuvier-like sagacity, he reconstructed, out of a hundred and twelve separate, minute, and scattered pieces, the metrical inscription in which Damasus expressed his desire to be buried with them, but his fear of vexing their sacred ashes.
12372-8{_}1408{_}[O]  [Footnote O: In another part of the catacombs the remainder of the stone that had been set over the grave of Cornelius was found.
12372-8{_}1409{_}It fitted precisely the piece first found by De Rossi.
12372-8{_}1410{_}The letters upon it were CORN EP.
12372-8{_}1411{_}The whole inscription then read, "Cornelius Martyr, Ep[iscopus.]"
12372-8{_}1412{_}It is rare that a bit of broken stone paves the way to such discoveries.
12372-8{_}1413{_}But it must be a man of genius who walks over the pavement.
12372-8{_}1414{_}Cardinal Wiseman has given an imperfect account of these discoveries in his diverting novel, _Fabiola_.]
12372-8{_}1415{_}There could no longer be any doubt; this was the Chapel of the Popes, and that of St. Cecilia must be near by.
12372-8{_}1416{_}Proceeding with the excavations, a door leading into a neighboring crypt was opened.
12372-8{_}1417{_}The crypt was filled with earth and _débris_, which appeared to have fallen into it through a _luminare_, now choked up with the growth and accumulated rubbish of centuries.
12372-8{_}1418{_}In order to remove the mass of earth with least risk of injury to the walls of the chamber, it was determined to take it out through the luminare from above.
12372-8{_}1419{_}As the work advanced, there were discovered on the wall of the luminare itself paintings of the figures of three men, with a name inscribed at the side of each,--Policamus, Sebastianus, and Cyrinus.
12372-8{_}1420{_}These names inspired fresh zeal, for they were those of saints who were mentioned in one or more of the itineraries as having been buried in the same chapel with St. Cecilia.
12372-8{_}1421{_}As the chapel was cleared, a large arcosolium was found, and near it a painting of a youthful woman, richly attired, adorned with necklaces and bracelets, and the dress altogether such as might befit a bride.
12372-8{_}1422{_}Below, on the same wall, was a figure of a pope in his robes, with the name "S[=e][=s] Urbanus" painted at the side: and close to this figure, a large head of the Saviour, of the Byzantine type, with a glory in the form of a Greek cross.
12372-8{_}1423{_}The character of the paintings showed that they were of comparatively late date, probably not earlier than the sixth century, and obviously executed at a time when the chapel was frequented by worshippers, and before the traditional knowledge of the exact site of St. Cecilia's sepulchre had been lost.
12372-8{_}1424{_}The discovery made by Paschal after the place had been deserted was thus repeated by De Rossi after a second, longer, and more obscure period of oblivion.
12372-8{_}1425{_}The divine vision which had led the ancient Pope, according to his own account, to the right spot, was now replaced by scientific investigation.
12372-8{_}1426{_}The statements of inspiration were confirmed, as in so many more conspicuous instances, by the discoveries of science.
12372-8{_}1427{_}Cecilia had lain so near the popes, that she might, as she had said to Paschal, have spoken to him when he was in their chapel, _as ad as_, "mouth to mouth."
12372-8{_}1428{_}But the questions naturally arose, Why was it that in Paschal's time, before this chapel was encumbered with earth, it had been so difficult to find her grave?
12372-8{_}1429{_}and, Why had not the Lombards, who had sought for her sacred body, succeeded in finding it?
12372-8{_}1430{_}De Rossi was able to furnish the solution.
12372-8{_}1431{_}In several instances he had found walls carefully built up in front of tombs so as to conceal them.
12372-8{_}1432{_}It was plain that this must have been done with some definite purpose; and it seems altogether likely that it was to hide these tombs from sacrilegious invaders.
12372-8{_}1433{_}The walls had been built when the faithful were forced by the presence of their enemies to desert the catacombs and leave them unprotected.
12372-8{_}1434{_}It was a striking illustration of the veneration in which these holy places had been held.
12372-8{_}1435{_}Upon examination of the floor in front of the areosolium of this chapel, traces of the foundation of a wall were discovered, and thus the Lombard failure and Paschal's difficulty were explained.
12372-8{_}1436{_}So ends the story of St Cecilia and her tomb.
12372-8{_}1437{_}Within her church are the remains of the bath-chamber where she suffered death.
12372-8{_}1438{_}The mosaics of the apse and the arch of triumph tell of the first finding of her body; Maderno's statue recalls the fact of its second discovery long after; and now this newly opened, long forgotten chapel shows where her precious body was first laid away in peace, brings the legend of her faithful death into clearer remembrance, and concludes the ancient story with dramatic and perfect completeness.
12372-8{_}1439{_}"The Lord discovereth deep things out of darkness, and bringeth out to light the shadow of death."
12372-8{_}1440{_}[To be continued.]
12372-8{_}1441{_}*       *       *       *       *   HAPPINESS.
12372-8{_}1442{_}Wing-Footed!
12372-8{_}1443{_}thou abid'st with him   That asks it not: but he who hath   Watched o'er the waves thy fading path   Will never more on ocean's rim,   At morn or eve, behold returning   Thy high-heaped canvas shoreward yearning:   Thou only teachest us the core   And inmost meaning of No More,   Thou, who first showest us thy face   Turned o'er the shoulder's parting grace,   And whose sad footprints we can trace   Away from every mortal door!
12372-8{_}1444{_}THE PURE PEARL OF DIVER'S BAY.
12372-8{_}1445{_}When the great storms raged along the Atlantic coast, they sometimes tossed a token into Diver's Bay.
12372-8{_}1446{_}In more than one of the rude cabins composing the fishermen's settlement memorials of shipwreck and disaster might be found; and these memorials did not always fail to kindle imagination, and to arouse soft feelings of pity for the calamities they suggested.
12372-8{_}1447{_}One morning, that dawned bright and mild after a week of tempest, Clarice Briton went out with her coarse basket to gather the sea-weed tossed on the shore.
12372-8{_}1448{_}She was the first child out that morning, and on account of the late storm, which had prevented the usual daily work, the harvest was a rich one.
12372-8{_}1449{_}There was always need that Clarice should work with her might when she found work to do, and she now labored from dawn till sunrise, filling her basket many times over, until the boards where she spread the weed to dry were nearly covered.
12372-8{_}1450{_}Then she threw herself down to rest by her father's door.
12372-8{_}1451{_}But when the sun was rising she went and sat among the rocks, and watched the changing of the sky and water, and the flocks of birds as they came screaming from their nests to dive among the waves and mount beyond her sight among the mists of morning.
12372-8{_}1452{_}She never tired of watching them, or of gazing on these scenes.
12372-8{_}1453{_}She knew the habits of the shore birds, understood their indications and devices, and whatever their movements foreboded concerning the weather.
12372-8{_}1454{_}Clarice was also versed in winds and clouds, and knew as well as the wise fishermen what the north-wind had in store, and what the south-wind would give them.
12372-8{_}1455{_}While she sat resting a few minutes, and wondering that the other children of the beach were so long in waking to the pleasant day, suddenly, as she looked down along the rocks that lay between her and the water, she saw lying near her feet, securely lodged by the waves among the stones, a basket.
12372-8{_}1456{_}It was a very different affair from that other, lying a few paces off, with which she went about gathering sea-weed.
12372-8{_}1457{_}It was small, and light, and delicately woven,--embroidered, too, with floss.
12372-8{_}1458{_}When she bent forward and picked it up, long strings of shiny weed dangled dripping from the handles,--and something beside; for, as she attempted to remove the traces of wild voyaging, something that was not weed resisted her efforts, and caused her to raise the lid.
12372-8{_}1459{_}As she did so, a chain, which had been partly secured by the closing of the lid, was disengaged, and fell into her lap.
12372-8{_}1460{_}"What's that, Clarice?"
12372-8{_}1461{_}said a voice just above her, as she in amazement lifted the chain, and endeavored to free it from the weed.
12372-8{_}1462{_}"Oh, Luke, there must have been a wreck!
12372-8{_}1463{_}See!
12372-8{_}1464{_}I found it just here at my feet," said Clarice, sorrowfully,--apparently not taken by surprise by the sudden coming and speaking of Luke Merlyn; she did not even lift her head, nor for an instant turn to him from what occupied her.
12372-8{_}1465{_}"There's a ring, too, I declare!"
12372-8{_}1466{_}said Luke, coming down to her side; and he took from her lap a small ring, in which was set a solitary pearl;--the ring had dropped from the chain.
12372-8{_}1467{_}"What next?
12372-8{_}1468{_}Look in."
12372-8{_}1469{_}Clarice opened the basket again, and turned out the white silk lining, which was soaking and stained with wild sea-travel.
12372-8{_}1470{_}"That is all," said she.
12372-8{_}1471{_}"That chain is a gold one," remarked Luke Merlyn.
12372-8{_}1472{_}"There must have been a wreck.
12372-8{_}1473{_}Who do you suppose these things belonged to?
12372-8{_}1474{_}Some lady?
12372-8{_}1475{_}Look at that basket now.
12372-8{_}1476{_}She kept her trinkets in it.
12372-8{_}1477{_}I suppose lots of 'em got shook out by the way.
12372-8{_}1478{_}I am glad it was you found it, Clarice.
12372-8{_}1479{_}Just try that ring on your finger now; I should think it might fit you."
12372-8{_}1480{_}He took up the ring and looked at Clarice, but she shrunk back shuddering.
12372-8{_}1481{_}"Oh, no!--I should feel as if it would drag me down to the bottom of the sea after the owner."
12372-8{_}1482{_}"It's the neatest thing I ever saw, though, Clarice.
12372-8{_}1483{_}Look, what a pearl!
12372-8{_}1484{_}You must keep it for your own, any way, if you won't wear it.
12372-8{_}1485{_}Nobody about here is fit but you.
12372-8{_}1486{_}The poor little basket, too,--poor little ark!"
12372-8{_}1487{_}He took it up and looked it over, much as though it were a dead bird, or some other pretty thing that once had life, and knew bow to enjoy it.
12372-8{_}1488{_}"Are you going out to-day, Luke?"
12372-8{_}1489{_}asked Clarice.
12372-8{_}1490{_}"Don't you see I've got the net?
12372-8{_}1491{_}Father will be down by the time I'm ready.
12372-8{_}1492{_}We are tired enough hanging about waiting for the blow to be over."
12372-8{_}1493{_}"May-be you will see something," said Clarice, in an undertone.
12372-8{_}1494{_}"If you could only find out about the ship, and the poor passengers!"
12372-8{_}1495{_}"May-be," answered Luke,--saying this to comfort her.
12372-8{_}1496{_}"Is your father going out to-day?"
12372-8{_}1497{_}"He said he would, last night.
12372-8{_}1498{_}I'm glad it came off so pleasant.
12372-8{_}1499{_}See how long this chain is!--a great many times longer than his big watch-chain!"
12372-8{_}1500{_}"Worth fifty times as much, too."
12372-8{_}1501{_}"Is it?"
12372-8{_}1502{_}said Clarice, looking up in wonder, almost incredulous;--but then Luke had said it.
12372-8{_}1503{_}"This is gold.
12372-8{_}1504{_}Come and walk down to the boat, Clarice.
12372-8{_}1505{_}How many times have you filled your basket this morning?
12372-8{_}1506{_}You look tired.
12372-8{_}1507{_}How did you come to wake up so soon?
12372-8{_}1508{_}I believe I heard you singing, and that was what brought me out so quick."
12372-8{_}1509{_}"I haven't sung any, Luke," she answered, looking at him in wonder.
12372-8{_}1510{_}"Oh, yes!--I'm sure I heard you.
12372-8{_}1511{_}I got up and looked out of my window; there you were.
12372-8{_}1512{_}You are the best girl around, Clarice!
12372-8{_}1513{_}Come now, why don't you say I'm the best fellow?
12372-8{_}1514{_}Then we'll be even.
12372-8{_}1515{_}I am, you know.
12372-8{_}1516{_}But then I want to hear you say so."
12372-8{_}1517{_}The merry fellow was in earnest, though he laughed.
12372-8{_}1518{_}He blushed more deeply than the girl,--indeed, she did not blush at all,--when he thus spoke to her.
12372-8{_}1519{_}She looked at him a little surprised.
12372-8{_}1520{_}"Come," said he, with gentle coaxing.
12372-8{_}1521{_}"I know what you think.
12372-8{_}1522{_}Speak out, and make me feel happy, all the days of my life.
12372-8{_}1523{_}If it wasn't that you feel so about the ring--But why shouldn't you feel solemn about it?
12372-8{_}1524{_}It belonged to some beautiful lady, I suppose, who lies at rest in the bottom of the sea by this time.
12372-8{_}1525{_}_H.H._"--he read the initials engraved on the clasp of the chain.
12372-8{_}1526{_}Clarice, who held the ring, inadvertently turned it that moment to the light so that her eyes could not fail to perceive that two letters were also written by a graver underneath the pearl.
12372-8{_}1527{_}These letters likewise were _H.H._ She gave the ring, to Luke, pointing to the initials.
12372-8{_}1528{_}"Yes, to be sure," said he, examining it with his bright eyes.
12372-8{_}1529{_}"It's the prettiest thing I ever saw.
12372-8{_}1530{_}These letters must have stood for something.
12372-8{_}1531{_}Clarice,"--he hesitated a moment,--"Clarice, they might stand for something yet, _Heart and Hand_.
12372-8{_}1532{_}Here they are,--take them,--they're yours,--my heart and my hand,--till Death comes between!"
12372-8{_}1533{_}"Don't talk that way, Luke," answered the girl, gravely.
12372-8{_}1534{_}"Your father is waiting for you, I'm sure."
12372-8{_}1535{_}But Luke did not believe that she was in such haste to be rid of him.
12372-8{_}1536{_}"He hasn't gone down yet.
12372-8{_}1537{_}I've watched," said he.
12372-8{_}1538{_}"He'd be willing to wait, if he knew what I was saying.
12372-8{_}1539{_}Besides, if you are in a hurry, it won't take but a minute to say yes, Clarice.
12372-8{_}1540{_}Will you take my heart and my hand?
12372-8{_}1541{_}Here is your ring."
12372-8{_}1542{_}Clarice took the ring and looked away; but, in looking away, her eyes fell on Luke, and she smiled.
12372-8{_}1543{_}"It's the prettiest thing, that ring is, in the world, except you, Clarice,"--so the smile made him speak.
12372-8{_}1544{_}"That's new for me," said the girl.
12372-8{_}1545{_}"Talk sense, Luke."
12372-8{_}1546{_}"Handsome is that handsome does, say I.
12372-8{_}1547{_}And if you a'n't the best girl in the Bay, Clary, who is, then?
12372-8{_}1548{_}When are you going to say yes?"
12372-8{_}1549{_}demanded the young fellow.
12372-8{_}1550{_}"Now," replied Clarice, suddenly.
12372-8{_}1551{_}"Have you taken my heart and hand?"
12372-8{_}1552{_}asked the lad as quickly, his face glowing with delight.
12372-8{_}1553{_}"Yes."
12372-8{_}1554{_}"To keep forever, Clarice?"
12372-8{_}1555{_}It seemed, after all, incredible.
12372-8{_}1556{_}"Yes, Luke."
12372-8{_}1557{_}And so speaking, the girl meant _yes, forever_.
12372-8{_}1558{_}Now this promise had not really taken either of these children by surprise.
12372-8{_}1559{_}They had long understood each other.
12372-8{_}1560{_}But when they had given a mutual promise, both looked grave.
12372-8{_}1561{_}Clarice stood by the water's edge, careless that time was passing.
12372-8{_}1562{_}Luke was in no hurry for his father.
12372-8{_}1563{_}But at length a shrill voice called the girl.
12372-8{_}1564{_}Dame Briton stood in the cabin door, and her angry tongue was laden with reproaches ready for utterance when Clarice should come within easier reach of her voice.
12372-8{_}1565{_}"I must go," said Clarice to Luke.
12372-8{_}1566{_}"I'll follow you, to-night.
12372-8{_}1567{_}Don't work too hard," he answered.
12372-8{_}1568{_}"Take care of my heart, Clarice."
12372-8{_}1569{_}A storm broke upon Clarice when she went home to her mother.
12372-8{_}1570{_}She bore the blame of her idleness with tolerable patience, until it seemed as if the gale would never blow over.
12372-8{_}1571{_}At last some quick words escaped her:--  "Three bushels of weed lie there on the boards ready spread, and drying.
12372-8{_}1572{_}I gathered them before another creature was stirring in Diver's Bay."
12372-8{_}1573{_}Then she added, more gently, "I found something besides."
12372-8{_}1574{_}But though Dame Briton heard, she passed this last bit of information without remark.
12372-8{_}1575{_}"Idling down there on the beach to see the boys off fishing!"
12372-8{_}1576{_}she could not help saying.
12372-8{_}1577{_}"You needn't be up afore the break o' day for work like that."
12372-8{_}1578{_}"It was Luke Merlyn."
12372-8{_}1579{_}"No matter."
12372-8{_}1580{_}"I showed him what I had found.
12372-8{_}1581{_}Ask him if I'm ever too free.
12372-8{_}1582{_}He'd know as quick as anybody,--and care as much."
12372-8{_}1583{_}Clarice, while speaking this, had departed yet farther both in look and voice from her usual serenity.
12372-8{_}1584{_}The dame let her last words pass without taking them up.
12372-8{_}1585{_}She was by this time curious.
12372-8{_}1586{_}"What did you find?"
12372-8{_}1587{_}asked she.
12372-8{_}1588{_}Clarice showed the basket and the gold chain.
12372-8{_}1589{_}Her mother handled both with wondering admiration, asking many a question.
12372-8{_}1590{_}At last she threw the chain around her neck.
12372-8{_}1591{_}"It's gold," said she.
12372-8{_}1592{_}"It's worth much.
12372-8{_}1593{_}If you could pick up the like of that every day, you might let the old weed-basket drift."
12372-8{_}1594{_}"I had rather gather weeds till my back was broken doing it, than ever find another," said Clarice.
12372-8{_}1595{_}The dame took this for a child's exaggeration; observing which, Clarice said, sadly,--  "Why, don't you see how it came to shore?
12372-8{_}1596{_}There's been a wreck in the storm last week.
12372-8{_}1597{_}Oh, may-be I've found all that will tell of it!"
12372-8{_}1598{_}"What's that in your hand?"
12372-8{_}1599{_}asked the dame, who spied the ring.
12372-8{_}1600{_}Clarice half opened her palm; she did not like to let the ring pass from her keeping, and all this while she had stood doubting whether or not she should show it to her mother.
12372-8{_}1601{_}Dame Briton took it quickly.
12372-8{_}1602{_}The dull glitter of greedy eyes fell on the mild lustre of the pearl, but found no reflection.
12372-8{_}1603{_}"A ring!"
12372-8{_}1604{_}said she, and she tried to fit it to her little finger.
12372-8{_}1605{_}It would not pass the first rough joint.
12372-8{_}1606{_}"Try it," said she to Clarice.
12372-8{_}1607{_}"No," was the quiet answer.
12372-8{_}1608{_}"But I will keep the ring.
12372-8{_}1609{_}It must have been a lady's.
12372-8{_}1610{_}May-be it was a token."
12372-8{_}1611{_}"May-be it was.--If your father should take that chain to the Port, he might make a handsome bargain,--if he was worth a snap at bargains.--Here's something; what be these marks?
12372-8{_}1612{_}look here, Clarice."
12372-8{_}1613{_}The face of the girl flushed a little as she answered,--"_H.
12372-8{_}1614{_}H_."
12372-8{_}1615{_}"_H.H.
12372-8{_}1616{_}!_ What does that mean?
12372-8{_}1617{_}I wonder."
12372-8{_}1618{_}"May-be the name of the owner," answered Clarice, timidly.
12372-8{_}1619{_}She was thinking, not of what the letters might have meant to others, but of what they had come to signify to her and Luke.
12372-8{_}1620{_}"Who knows?"
12372-8{_}1621{_}answered her mother; and she stood musing and absent, and her face had a solemn look.
12372-8{_}1622{_}Clarice now took the basket to the fireplace and held it there till it was dried.
12372-8{_}1623{_}With the drying the colors brightened and the sand was easily brushed away; but many a stain remained on the once dainty white silk lining; the basket would hardly have been recognized by its owner.
12372-8{_}1624{_}Having dried and cleansed it as well as she was able, Clarice laid it away in a chest for safe-keeping, and then ate her breakfast, standing.
12372-8{_}1625{_}After that, she went out to work again until the tide should come in.
12372-8{_}1626{_}She left the chain with her mother, but the ring she had tied to a cord, and hung it around her neck.
12372-8{_}1627{_}By this time the children of the fishermen were all out, and the most industrious of them at work.
12372-8{_}1628{_}They scattered among the rocks and crags, and wandered up and down the coast three miles, gathering sea-weed, which it was their custom to dry, and then carry to town, the Port, not many miles distant, where it was purchased by the glassmakers.
12372-8{_}1629{_}Clarice had neither brother nor sister, and she made little of the children of the neighboring fishermen; for her life was one of toil, and her inheritance seemed very different from theirs, though they were all poor, and ate the crusts of labor.
12372-8{_}1630{_}Her father, had Nature only given him what she seemed to have intended at the outset, might have been as successful a fisherman as lived at the Bay.
12372-8{_}1631{_}But he trusted to luck, and contrived to make half of what he earned a serious damage to him.
12372-8{_}1632{_}The remainder was little enough for the comfort of his family, small though that family was.
12372-8{_}1633{_}Briton was a good fellow, everybody said.
12372-8{_}1634{_}They meant that he was always ready for sport, and time-wasting, and drinking, and that sort of generosity which is the shabbiest sort of selfishness.
12372-8{_}1635{_}They called him "Old Briton," but he was not, by many, the oldest man in Diver's Bay; he might have been the wickedest, had he not been the jolliest, and incapable of hiding malice in his heart.
12372-8{_}1636{_}And if I said he was out and out the wickedest, I should request that people would refrain from lifting up their hands in horror, on account of the poor old fellow.
12372-8{_}1637{_}We all know--alas, perhaps, we all love--wickeder souls than could have been produced from among the older fishermen, had all their sins been concentrated in one individual.
12372-8{_}1638{_}Old Briton was what the people called a lucky fisherman.
12372-8{_}1639{_}In seasons when he chose to work, the result was sufficiently obvious, to himself and others, to astonish both.
12372-8{_}1640{_}But even in the best seasons he was a bad manager.
12372-8{_}1641{_}He trusted everybody, and found, to his astonishment, how few deserve to be trusted.
12372-8{_}1642{_}Dame Briton was a stout, loud-talking woman, whom experience had not softened in her ways of speech or thought or action.
12372-8{_}1643{_}She was generally at strife with her husband, but the strife was most illogical.
12372-8{_}1644{_}It did not admit of a single legitimate deduction in the mind of a third person.
12372-8{_}1645{_}It seemed sometimes as if the pair were possessed of the instincts of those animals which unite for mutual destruction, and as if their purpose were to fulfil their destiny with the utmost rapidity.
12372-8{_}1646{_}In the years when Dame Briton, by nature proud and ambitious, was putting forth the most successful efforts she ever made at decent housekeeping, endeavoring to transform her husband into such a person as he was not born to be, striving hard to work her will,--in those years Clarice was born.
12372-8{_}1647{_}Is the pearl a product of disease?
12372-8{_}1648{_}Clarice grew up in the midst of influences not the purest or most elevating.
12372-8{_}1649{_}She was not by nature gay, but silent, truthful, and industrious.
12372-8{_}1650{_}She was no coward by nature, and her training made her brave and hardy.
12372-8{_}1651{_}Sometimes Old Briton called her his boy, and exacted from her the service of a son.
12372-8{_}1652{_}Dame Briton did not quarrel with him for that; she was as proud as the fisherman of any feat of skill or strength or courage performed by Clarice.
12372-8{_}1653{_}In their way they were both fond of the child, but their fondness had strange manifestation; and of much tender speech, or fondling, or praise, the girl stood in no danger.
12372-8{_}1654{_}Idleness especially was held up before her, from the outset, as the most destructive evil and dire iniquity of which human creature was capable; and Old Briton, lounging about all day with his pipe in his mouth,--by no means a rare spectacle,--did not interfere with the lesson the child's mother enforced.
12372-8{_}1655{_}Winter and summer there was enough for the little feet and hands to do.
12372-8{_}1656{_}So, as Clarice grew up, she earned the best reputation for industry of any girl in Diver's Bay.
12372-8{_}1657{_}Before she became the praise of the serious Bay people, Luke Merlyn's bright eyes were on the little girl, and he had a settled habit of seeking times and opportunities for quiet talks with her.
12372-8{_}1658{_}He liked to ask and follow her advice in many matters.
12372-8{_}1659{_}Many a heavy basket of weeds had he helped her carry home from the rocks; many a shell and pebble had he picked up in his coast-work, when he went beyond the limits of the Bay,--because he knew the good girl had a liking for every pretty thing.
12372-8{_}1660{_}If Clarice Briton was the finest girl, Luke Merlyn, beyond question, was the most promising fellow in this little village of fishermen.
12372-8{_}1661{_}He was strong, active, ready for any undertaking that required a bold spirit and firm hand,--was quicker in thought and readier in speech than any lad about.
12372-8{_}1662{_}He had a little personal vanity,--and good looks to encourage the same; but he had besides a generous heart, and the conviction was general, whether expressed or not, that in Luke a man was growing up who would some day take the lead among the fishermen of Diver's Bay.
12372-8{_}1663{_}He had a livelier fancy, a more active imagination, than any lad thereabout; these qualities of mind, united to his courage and warmth of heart, seemed to point toward a future worth arriving at.
12372-8{_}1664{_}II.
12372-8{_}1665{_}When Luke returned from fishing, towards evening, he went down to Briton's cabin, hardly taking time to remove from his person the traces of his day of toil, his haste was so great.
12372-8{_}1666{_}Briton had arrived before him, and now sat at supper with his cup of grog beside him.
12372-8{_}1667{_}When Luke entered, Dame Briton was exhibiting the gold chain, reserved, in spite of her impatience, till she had cooked the supper.
12372-8{_}1668{_}It was partly on account of this chain that Luke had made such haste in coming.
12372-8{_}1669{_}He felt interested in the fortunes of the family to-night, and he knew Briton's habit of bargaining and throwing away treasure.
12372-8{_}1670{_}Clarice was standing on the hearth when he arrived.
12372-8{_}1671{_}As Luke passed the window, he thought her face looked very sad; but when he crossed the threshold, the expression greatly changed, or else he was mistaken.
12372-8{_}1672{_}She had been telling her father how she found the chain,--but concerning the ring was silent, as in the morning.
12372-8{_}1673{_}That ring was still fastened to its cord, and hung about her neck.
12372-8{_}1674{_}With reluctance she had shown it even to her mother, and by this time, having scarcely thought of anything beside, it possessed an almost sacred charm to her eyes.
12372-8{_}1675{_}Why should I not say it was the most sacred of all things to her, since that is but true?
12372-8{_}1676{_}"Is that the chain," asked Luke, as he came up behind the fisherman's chair, and clapped Old Briton on the shoulder.
12372-8{_}1677{_}"You could trade that for a silver watch."
12372-8{_}1678{_}"What's that?"
12372-8{_}1679{_}asked Briton, quickly taking up the lad's words; and he pulled out his pewter watch and laid it on the table.
12372-8{_}1680{_}"A silver watch?"
12372-8{_}1681{_}said he.
12372-8{_}1682{_}"A silver watch, as good as ever run, for that gold chain.
12372-8{_}1683{_}Just see how fine it is!"
12372-8{_}1684{_}"So, so!"
12372-8{_}1685{_}said the fisherman, thoughtfully resting his rough chin in his broad palm.
12372-8{_}1686{_}That was his attitude, when, at home, he contemplated any of those famous bargains which always turned out so differently from anything that he anticipated.
12372-8{_}1687{_}"Let Luke do the trading for ye," said Briton's wife, quickly recognizing his symptoms.
12372-8{_}1688{_}She looked from the lad to her daughter, and back again, five or six times in a second,--seeing more than most people could have seen in observation apparently so careless and superficial.
12372-8{_}1689{_}"I kept a sharp look out, Clary, all day, but I saw nothing," said Luke, going over to the hearth.
12372-8{_}1690{_}"Nothing,--but," he added, she looked so disappointed, "but, for all that, some one else may."
12372-8{_}1691{_}"Oh, I hope so"!"
12372-8{_}1692{_}"What are you talking about?"
12372-8{_}1693{_}asked Briton.
12372-8{_}1694{_}"The shipwreck," said Luke.
12372-8{_}1695{_}"Oh!--well, Luke,--will you make the trade, Sir?
12372-8{_}1696{_}What do _you_ say, Clarice?
12372-8{_}1697{_}The chain belongs to you, after all," said Briton, with a laugh,--he could not help the shipwreck.
12372-8{_}1698{_}"What are you going to do with it, my girl?"
12372-8{_}1699{_}"It is yours, father."
12372-8{_}1700{_}"Thank ye!--a present!"
12372-8{_}1701{_}Old Briton looked well pleased.
12372-8{_}1702{_}"And if Luke will take it over"--  "I'll go to-night," said Luke, ready to start that moment, if such was the wish of any person in the house.
12372-8{_}1703{_}Briton laughed.
12372-8{_}1704{_}"No, you won't," said he.
12372-8{_}1705{_}"What the deuse!--Sit down and take something.
12372-8{_}1706{_}What are you all standing about for?
12372-8{_}1707{_}Sit down.
12372-8{_}1708{_}You shall do the trading, Luke.
12372-8{_}1709{_}There now, I've said it, and I hope you are all easy."
12372-8{_}1710{_}He laughed again; for he knew very well--he had often enough heard it stated in full--the estimate set on his skill in making a bargain.
12372-8{_}1711{_}"You haven't seen the ring yet?"
12372-8{_}1712{_}said Dame Briton, quite kindly, now that this matter was settled to her mind.
12372-8{_}1713{_}"Where's the ring, Clarice?"
12372-8{_}1714{_}Other eyes were on the girl besides those of her mother.
12372-8{_}1715{_}Old Briton pushed back his dish, and looked at Clarice.
12372-8{_}1716{_}Luke was smiling.
12372-8{_}1717{_}That smile became joyful and beautiful to see, when Clarice, blushing, removed the string from her neck and showed the ring.
12372-8{_}1718{_}"That's neat," said Briton, turning the delicate ornament round and round, examining its chaste workmanship admiringly.
12372-8{_}1719{_}"I never saw a pearl like that, Mother.
12372-8{_}1720{_}What do you wear it round your neck for, Clarice?--put it on your finger."
12372-8{_}1721{_}Luke Merlyn had come to Briton's cabin to explain how matters stood between him and Clarice, as well as to look after the other bargain.
12372-8{_}1722{_}Taking advantage of her hesitation, he now said,--  "She could not wear it at her work.
12372-8{_}1723{_}And it's a token betwixt her and me.
12372-8{_}1724{_}_Heart and Hand_.
12372-8{_}1725{_}Don't you see the letters?
12372-8{_}1726{_}That's what they mean to us."
12372-8{_}1727{_}Luke spoke out so boldly, that Clarice ceased to tremble; and when he took her hand and held it, she was satisfied to stand there and answer, that the joined hands were a symbol of the united hearts.
12372-8{_}1728{_}"What's that, old woman?"
12372-8{_}1729{_}asked Briton, looking at his wife, as if for an explanation.
12372-8{_}1730{_}"Luke, what do you mean?
12372-8{_}1731{_}Are you asking for Clarice?"
12372-8{_}1732{_}inquired the dame.
12372-8{_}1733{_}"Yes, Mrs.
12372-8{_}1734{_}Briton."
12372-8{_}1735{_}"That's right enough, old woman," said Briton; and strong approval, together with some emotion, was in his voice.
12372-8{_}1736{_}"Babes in arms, both of 'em!
12372-8{_}1737{_}But a promise a'n't no hurt,"--was the dame's comment.
12372-8{_}1738{_}Neither was she quite unmoved, as she looked at the young pair standing on the hearth; such another, her heart told her, was not to be found in Diver's Bay.
12372-8{_}1739{_}"Clarice is a good girl, Luke Merlyn," said Old Briton, solemnly.
12372-8{_}1740{_}"She is so," confirmed the mother.
12372-8{_}1741{_}"So take the ring there for your token."
12372-8{_}1742{_}Luke came forward and received the ring from Old Briton, and he laid the string that held it round Clarice's neck.
12372-8{_}1743{_}"Take this chain," said Briton, with a softened voice.
12372-8{_}1744{_}"It's fitter than the string, and none too good for Clarice.
12372-8{_}1745{_}Take it, Luke, and put the ring on't."
12372-8{_}1746{_}"I'm going to trade that chain for a silver watch," said Luke, answering according to the light he saw in the eyes of Clarice.
12372-8{_}1747{_}"That chain is Clary's wedding present to her father."
12372-8{_}1748{_}"Thank you, Luke," said Briton,--and he drew his hand across his eyes, not for a pretence.
12372-8{_}1749{_}Then he took up his old pewter watch, the companion of many years; he looked at it without and within, silently; perhaps was indulging in a little sentimental reflection; but he put it into his pocket without speaking, and went on with his supper, as if nothing had happened.
12372-8{_}1750{_}*       *       *       *       *  This took place before Clarice was fourteen years of age.
12372-8{_}1751{_}At seventeen she was still living under her father's roof, and between her and Luke Merlyn the pearl ring still remained a token.
12372-8{_}1752{_}Luke used to praise her beauty when there was little of it to praise.
12372-8{_}1753{_}He was not blinder when the young face began to be conspicuous for the growing loveliness of the spirit within.
12372-8{_}1754{_}The little slender figure sprang up into larger, fuller life, with vigor, strength, and grace; the activity of her thoughts and the brightness of their intelligence became evident, as well as the tenderness and courage of her heart.
12372-8{_}1755{_}Her own home, and many another, was the better for Clarice.
12372-8{_}1756{_}Some Sunday in this summer of her seventeenth year, when the missionary came down to the Bay, they were to be married.
12372-8{_}1757{_}It was settled where they were to live.
12372-8{_}1758{_}A few years before, a young artist came to the Bay and built a cabin near the settlement; there, during the summer months, he lodged, for several seasons,--spending his time in studying the rocks of the coast and sailing about in his pleasure-boat.
12372-8{_}1759{_}The last autumn he spent here he gave the cabin to Luke, in consideration of some generous service, and it was well known that to this home Luke would bring his wife ere long.
12372-8{_}1760{_}III.
12372-8{_}1761{_}But one bright day of this gay summer of anticipated bridal, Luke Merlyn went with his father, taking the fishing-nets, and a dozen men beside sailed or rowed out from the moorings; and all that went returned, save Merlyn and his son,--returned alive, but rowing desperately, sails furled, rowing for life in the gale.
12372-8{_}1762{_}Nearly all the women and children of the Bay were down on the beach at nightfall, watching for the coming of husband, son, and brother; and before dark all had arrived except Merlyn and his Luke.
12372-8{_}1763{_}The wind was blowing with terrific violence, and darkness fell on the deep like despair.
12372-8{_}1764{_}But until the windows of heaven were opened, and the floods poured down, Clarice Briton and her father, and the wife and children of Merlyn, stood on the beach, or climbed the rocks, and waited and tried to watch.
12372-8{_}1765{_}There was little sleep among them all that night.
12372-8{_}1766{_}With the first approach of day, Clarice, who had sat all night by the fire watching with her fears, was out again waiting till dawn should enable her to search the shore.
12372-8{_}1767{_}She was not long alone.
12372-8{_}1768{_}The fishermen gathered together, and when they saw the poor girl who had come before them, for her sake they comforted each other, as men dare,--and for her sake, more than their own, when they saw that there had come in to shore by night no token of disaster.
12372-8{_}1769{_}Doubtless, they argued, Merlyn had put into the nearest port when the sudden storm arose.
12372-8{_}1770{_}As the day advanced, they one after another got out their boats, and rowed down the bay, but did not take their nets.
12372-8{_}1771{_}Bondo Emmins went out with Old Briton, and Clarice heard him say, though he did not address her, that, if Luke Merlyn was alive, they would never come home without him.
12372-8{_}1772{_}Now Bondo Emmins never loved Luke Merlyn, for Luke won every prize that Bondo coveted; and Bondo was not a hero to admire such superior skill.
12372-8{_}1773{_}When Clarice heard his words, and saw that he was going out with her father, her heart stood still; it did not bless him; she turned away quickly, faint, cold, shivering.
12372-8{_}1774{_}What he said had to her ears the sound of an assurance that this search was vain.
12372-8{_}1775{_}All day there was sad waiting, weary watching, around Diver's Bay.
12372-8{_}1776{_}And late in the afternoon but one or two of the boats that went out in search had returned.
12372-8{_}1777{_}Towards evening Clarice walked away to the Point, three miles off; thence she could watch the boats as they approached the Bay from the ocean.
12372-8{_}1778{_}Once before, that day, under the scorching noontide sun, she had gone thither,--and now again, for she could not endure the sympathy of friends or the wondering watch of curious eyes.
12372-8{_}1779{_}It was better than to stand and wait,--better than to face the grief of Merlyn's wife and children,--better than to see the pity in her neighbors' faces, or even than to hear the voice of her own mother.
12372-8{_}1780{_}The waves had freight for her that evening.
12372-8{_}1781{_}When the tide came in, and her eyes were lifted, gazing afar, scanning the broad expanse of water with such searching, anxious vision, as, it seemed, nothing could escape, Luke Merlyn's cap was dashed to her very feet, tossed from the grave.
12372-8{_}1782{_}Moving back to escape the encroaching tide, Clarice saw the cap lying, caught on the cragged point of rock before her.
12372-8{_}1783{_}Oh, she knew it well!
12372-8{_}1784{_}She stooped,--she took it up,--she need not wait for any other token.
12372-8{_}1785{_}She dared not look upon the sea again.
12372-8{_}1786{_}She turned away.
12372-8{_}1787{_}But whither?
12372-8{_}1788{_}Where now was her home?
12372-8{_}1789{_}So long a time, since she was a child, it had been in the heart of Luke!
12372-8{_}1790{_}Where was that heart lying?
12372-8{_}1791{_}What meant this token sent to her from the deep sea?
12372-8{_}1792{_}Oh, life and love!
12372-8{_}1793{_}was not all now over?
12372-8{_}1794{_}Heart still, hand powerless, home lost, she sat on the beach till night fell.
12372-8{_}1795{_}At sunset she stood up to look once more up and down the mighty field of waters, along the shore, as far as her eyes could reach,--but saw nothing.
12372-8{_}1796{_}Then she sat down again, and waited until long after the stars appeared.
12372-8{_}1797{_}Once or twice the thought that her mother would wonder at her long absence moved her; but she impatiently controlled the feeble impulse to arise and return, until she recalled the words of Bondo Emmins.
12372-8{_}1798{_}Luke's mother, too,--and the cap in her care.
12372-8{_}1799{_}If no one else had tidings for her, she had tidings.
12372-8{_}1800{_}Her father had reached home before her, and there was now no watcher on the beach, so far as Clarice could discover.
12372-8{_}1801{_}Perhaps there was no longer any doubt in any mind.
12372-8{_}1802{_}She hurried to the cabin.
12372-8{_}1803{_}At the door she met Bondo Emmins coming out.
12372-8{_}1804{_}He had a lantern in his hand.
12372-8{_}1805{_}"Is that you, Clarice?"
12372-8{_}1806{_}said he.
12372-8{_}1807{_}"I was just going to look for you."
12372-8{_}1808{_}She scanned his face by the glare of the lantern with terrible eagerness, to see what tidings he had for her.
12372-8{_}1809{_}He only looked grave.
12372-8{_}1810{_}It was a face whose signs Clarice had never wholly trusted, but she did not doubt them now.
12372-8{_}1811{_}"I have found his cap," said she, in a low, troubled voice.
12372-8{_}1812{_}"You said, that, if he was alive, you would find him.
12372-8{_}1813{_}I heard you.
12372-8{_}1814{_}What have you found?"
12372-8{_}1815{_}"Nothing."
12372-8{_}1816{_}Then she passed by him, though he would have spoken further.
12372-8{_}1817{_}She went into the house and sat down on the hearth with Luke's cap in her hand, which she held up before the fire to dry.
12372-8{_}1818{_}So she sat one morning holding the tiny basket which the waves had dashed ashore.
12372-8{_}1819{_}Briton and his wife looked at each other, and at young Emmins, who, after a moment's hesitation, had put out the lantern light, and followed her back into the house.
12372-8{_}1820{_}"It is his cap," said Bondo, in a low voice, but not so low as to escape the ear of Clarice.
12372-8{_}1821{_}"The sea sent it for a token," said she, without turning her gaze from the fire.
12372-8{_}1822{_}The old people moved up to the hearth.
12372-8{_}1823{_}"Sit down, Emmins," said Briton.
12372-8{_}1824{_}"You've served us well to-day."
12372-8{_}1825{_}In any trouble Old Briton's comfort was in feeling a stout wall of flesh around him.
12372-8{_}1826{_}Bondo sat down.
12372-8{_}1827{_}Then he and Briton helped each other explain the course taken by themselves and the other boat-men that day, and they talked of what they would do on the morrow; but they failed to comfort Clarice, or to awaken in her any hope.
12372-8{_}1828{_}She knew that in reality they had no hope themselves.
12372-8{_}1829{_}"They will never come back," said she.
12372-8{_}1830{_}"You will never find them."
12372-8{_}1831{_}She spoke so calmly that her father was deceived.
12372-8{_}1832{_}If this was her conviction, it would be safe to speak his own.
12372-8{_}1833{_}"The tide may bring the poor fellows in," said he.
12372-8{_}1834{_}At these words the cap which the poor girl held fell from her hand.
12372-8{_}1835{_}She spoke no more.
12372-8{_}1836{_}No word or cry escaped her,--not by a look did she acknowledge that there was community in this grief,--as solitary as if she were alone in the universe, she sat gazing into the fire.
12372-8{_}1837{_}She was not overcome by things external, tangible, as she had been when she sat alone out on the sea-beach at the Point.
12372-8{_}1838{_}The world in an instant seemed to sink out of her vision, and time from her consciousness; her soul set out on a search in which her mortal sense had failed,--and here no arm of flesh could help her.
12372-8{_}1839{_}"I shall find him," she said, in a whisper.
12372-8{_}1840{_}They all heard her, and looked at one another, trouble and wonder in their faces.
12372-8{_}1841{_}"I shall find him," she repeated, in a louder tone; and she drew herself up, and bent forward,--but her eyes saw not the cheerful fire-light, her ears took in no sound of crackling fagot, rising wind, or muttered fear among the three who sat and looked at her.
12372-8{_}1842{_}Bondo Emmins had taken up the cap when Clarice dropped it,--he had examined it inside and out, and passed it to Dame Briton.
12372-8{_}1843{_}There was no mistaking the ownership.
12372-8{_}1844{_}Not a child of Diver's Bay but would have recognized it as the property of Luke Merlyn.
12372-8{_}1845{_}The dame passed it to the old man, who looked at it through tears, and then smoothed it over his great fist, and came nearer to the fire, and silence fell upon them all.
12372-8{_}1846{_}At last Dame Briton said, beginning stoutly, but ending with a sob, "Has anybody seen poor Merlyn's wife?
12372-8{_}1847{_}Who'll tell her?
12372-8{_}1848{_}Oh!
12372-8{_}1849{_}oh!"
12372-8{_}1850{_}"I will go tell her that Clarice found the cap," said Bondo Emmins, rising.
12372-8{_}1851{_}Clarice sat like one in a stupor,--but, that was no dull light shining from her eyes.
12372-8{_}1852{_}Still she seemed deaf and dumb; for, when Bondo bade her good-night, she did not answer him, nor give the slightest intimation that she was aware of what passed around her.
12372-8{_}1853{_}But when he was gone, and her father said,--"Come, Clarice,--now for bed,--you'll wake the earlier,"--she instantly arose to act on his suggestion.
12372-8{_}1854{_}He followed her to the door of her little chamber and lingered there a moment.
12372-8{_}1855{_}He wanted to say something for comfort, but had nothing to say; so he turned away in silence, and drank a pint of grog.
12372-8{_}1856{_}IV.
12372-8{_}1857{_}Bondo Emmins was not a native of Diver's Bay.
12372-8{_}1858{_}Only during the past three or four years had he lived among the fishermen.
12372-8{_}1859{_}He called the place his home, but now and then indications of restlessness escaped him, and seemed to promise years of wandering, rather than a life of patient, contented industry.
12372-8{_}1860{_}He and Luke Merlyn were as unlike as any two young men that ever fished in the same bay.
12372-8{_}1861{_}Luke was as firm, constant, reliable, from the day when he first managed a net, as any veteran whose gray hairs are honorable.
12372-8{_}1862{_}Emmins flashed here and there like a wandering star; and whatever people might say of him when he was out of sight, he had the art of charming them to admiration while they were under his personal influence.
12372-8{_}1863{_}He was lavish with his money; almost every cabin had a gift from him.
12372-8{_}1864{_}He could talk forever, and with many was a true oracle.
12372-8{_}1865{_}Though he worked regularly at his business, work seemed turned to play when he took it in hand.
12372-8{_}1866{_}He could shout so as to be heard across the ocean,--so the children thought; he told stories better than any; and at the signal of his laughter it seemed as if the walls themselves would shake to pieces.
12372-8{_}1867{_}When he hit on a device, it was strange indeed if he did not succeed in executing it; and no one was the wiser for the mortification and inward displeasure of the man, when he failed in any enterprise.
12372-8{_}1868{_}When Emmins came to Diver's Bay Clarice Briton was but a child, yet already the promised wife of Luke Merlyn.
12372-8{_}1869{_}If this fact was made known to him, as very probably it was, Clarice was not a girl to excite his admiration or win his love.
12372-8{_}1870{_}But as time passed on, Emmins found that he was not the only man in Diver's Bay; of all men to regard as a rival, there was Luke Merlyn!
12372-8{_}1871{_}Luke, who went quietly about his business, interfering with no one, careful, brave, exact, had a firm place among the people, which might for a time be overshadowed, but from which he could not be moved.
12372-8{_}1872{_}Two or three times Bondo Emmins stumbled against that impregnable position, and found that he must take himself out of the way.
12372-8{_}1873{_}A small jealousy, a sharp rivalry, which no one suspected, quietly sprang up in his mind, and influenced his conduct; and he was not one who ever attempted to subdue or destroy what he found within him, he was instead always endeavoring to bring the outer world into harmony with what he found within.
12372-8{_}1874{_}A fine time he had of it, persistently laboring to make a victim of himself to himself!
12372-8{_}1875{_}People praised Clarice Briton, and now and then Emmins looked that way, and saw that the girl, indeed, was well enough.
12372-8{_}1876{_}He despised Luke, and Clarice seemed a very proper match for him.
12372-8{_}1877{_}But while Bondo Emmins was managing in his own way, and cherishing the feeling he had against Luke, by seeking to prove himself the braver and more skilful fellow, Clarice was growing older in years and in love, her soul was growing brighter, her heart was getting lighter, her mind clearer,--her womanhood was unfolding in a certain lovely manner that was discernible to other eyes than those of Luke Merlyn.
12372-8{_}1878{_}Luke said it was the ring that wrought the change,--that he could see its light all around her,--that it had a charm of which they could know nothing save by its results, for its secret had perished with its owner in the sea.
12372-8{_}1879{_}His mermaid he would sometimes call her,--and declared that often, by that mysterious pearly light, he saw Clarice when far out at sea, and that at any time by two words he could bring her to him.
12372-8{_}1880{_}She knew the words,--they were as dear to her as to him.
12372-8{_}1881{_}While Clarice was thus unfolding to this loveliness through love, Bondo Emmins suddenly saw her as if for the first time.
12372-8{_}1882{_}The vision was to him as surprising as if the ring had indeed a power of enchantment, and it had been thrown around him.
12372-8{_}1883{_}He was as active and as resolute in attempting to persuade himself that all this was nothing to him as he was active and resolute in other endeavors,--but he was not as successful as he supposed he should be.
12372-8{_}1884{_}For it was not enough that Emmins should laugh at himself, and say that the pretty couple were meant for each other.
12372-8{_}1885{_}Now and then, by accident, he obtained a glimpse of Clarice's happy heart; the pearl-like secret of their love, which was none the less a secret because everybody knew that Luke and Clarice were to be married some day, would sometimes of itself unexpectedly give some token, which he, it seemed, could better appreciate than any one beside the parties concerned.
12372-8{_}1886{_}When some such glimpse was obtained, some such token received, Bondo Emmins would retire within himself to a most gloomy seclusion; there was a world which had been conquered, and therein he had no foothold.
12372-8{_}1887{_}If Clarice wore the pearl in her bosom, on Luke's head was a crown, and Bondo Emmins just hated him for that.
12372-8{_}1888{_}But he never thought of a very easy method by which he might have escaped the trouble of his jealousy.
12372-8{_}1889{_}The great highway of ocean was open before him, and millions of men beside Luke Merlyn were in the world, millions of women beside Clarice Briton.
12372-8{_}1890{_}No!
12372-8{_}1891{_}Diver's Bay,--and a score of people,--and a thought that smelt like brimstone, and fiery enough to burn through the soul that tried to keep it,--this for him;--fishing,--making bargains,--visiting at Old Briton's,--making presents to the dame,--telling stories, singing songs by that fireside, and growing quieter by every other,--that was the way he did it;--cured himself of jealousy?
12372-8{_}1892{_}No!
12372-8{_}1893{_}made himself a fool.
12372-8{_}1894{_}Old Briton liked this young man; he could appreciate his excellences even better than he could those of Luke; there were some points of resemblance between them.
12372-8{_}1895{_}Emmins was as careless of money, as indifferent to growing rich, as Briton ever was; the virtues of the youth were not such as ever reproached the vices of the veteran.
12372-8{_}1896{_}They could make boisterous merriment in each other's company.
12372-8{_}1897{_}Briton's praise was never lacking when Bondo's name was mentioned.
12372-8{_}1898{_}He accepted service of the youth, and the two were half the time working in partnership.
12372-8{_}1899{_}In the cabin he had always a welcome, and Dame Briton gave him her entire confidence.
12372-8{_}1900{_}Luke did not fear, he had once admired the man; and because he was a peace-maker by nature, and could himself keep the peace, he never took any of Bondo's scathing speech in anger nor remembered it against him.
12372-8{_}1901{_}Usually he joined in the laugh, unless some brave, manly word were required; honorable in his nature, he could not be always jealous in maintaining that of which he felt so secure.
12372-8{_}1902{_}If Clarice did not penetrate the cause, she clearly saw the fact that Bondo Emmins had no love for Luke.
12372-8{_}1903{_}She might wonder at it, but Luke suffered no loss in consequence,--it was rather to his praise, she thought, that this was so.
12372-8{_}1904{_}And she remembered the disputes between the young men which she had chanced to hear, only to decide again, as she had often decided, in favor of Luke's justice and truth.
12372-8{_}1905{_}When the time of great trouble came, and this man was going out with her father in search of Merlyn and his son, her impulse, had she acted on it, would have prevented him.
12372-8{_}1906{_}He looked so strong, so proud, in spite of his solemn face!
12372-8{_}1907{_}He looked so full of life, she could not endure to think that his eyes might discover the dead body of poor Luke.
12372-8{_}1908{_}When she came home and found that he had returned with her father, before her, on the evening of that day of vain search for Merlyn and his son, a strange satisfaction came to Clarice for a moment,--touched her heart and passed,--was gone as it came.
12372-8{_}1909{_}When she said, "I shall find him," conviction, as well as determination, was in the words,--and more beside than entered the ears of those that heard her.
12372-8{_}1910{_}[To be continued.]
12372-8{_}1911{_}THE STORY OF KARIN.
12372-8{_}1912{_}A DANISH LEGEND.
12372-8{_}1913{_}Karin the fair, Karin the gay,   She came on the morn of her bridal day,--    She came to the mill-pond clear and bright,   And viewed hersel' in the morning light.
12372-8{_}1914{_}"And oh," she cried, "that my bonny brow   May ever be white and smooth as now!
12372-8{_}1915{_}"And oh, my hair, that I love to braid,   Be yellow in sunshine, and brown in shade!
12372-8{_}1916{_}"And oh, my waist, sae slender and fine,   May it never need girdle longer than mine!"
12372-8{_}1917{_}She lingered and laughed o'er the waters clear,   When sudden she starts, and shrieks in fear:--    "Oh, what is this face, sae laidly old,   That looks at my side in the waters cold?"
12372-8{_}1918{_}She turns around to view the bank,   And the osier willows dark and dank;--    And from the fern she sees arise   An aged crone wi' awsome eyes,    "Ha!
12372-8{_}1919{_}ha!"
12372-8{_}1920{_}she laughed, "ye're a bonny bride!
12372-8{_}1921{_}See how ye'll fare gin the New Year tide!
12372-8{_}1922{_}"Ye'll wear a robe sae blithely gran',   An ell-long girdle canna span.
12372-8{_}1923{_}"When twal-months three shall pass away,   Your berry-brown hair shall be streaked wi' gray.
12372-8{_}1924{_}"And gin ye be mither of bairnies nine,   Your brow shall be wrinkled and dark as mine."
12372-8{_}1925{_}Karin she sprang to her feet wi' speed,   And clapped her hands abune her head:--    "I pray to the saints and spirits all   That never a child may me mither call!"
12372-8{_}1926{_}The crone drew near, and the crone she spake:--   "Nine times flesh and banes shall ache.
12372-8{_}1927{_}"Laidly and awsome ye shall wane   Wi' toil, and care, and travail-pain."
12372-8{_}1928{_}"Better," said Karin, "lay me low,   And sink for aye in the water's flow!"
12372-8{_}1929{_}The crone raised her withered hand on high,   And showed her a tree that stood hard by.
12372-8{_}1930{_}"And take of the bonny fruit," she said,   "And eat till the seeds are dark and red.
12372-8{_}1931{_}"Count them less, or count them more,   Nine times you shall number o'er;--    "And when each number you shall speak,   Cast seed by seed into the lake."
12372-8{_}1932{_}Karin she ate of the fruit sae fine;   'Twas mellow as sand, and sweet as brine.
12372-8{_}1933{_}Seed by seed she let them fall;   The waters rippled over all.
12372-8{_}1934{_}But ilka seed as Karin threw,   Uprose a bubble to her view,--    Uprose a sigh from out the lake,   As though a baby's heart did break.
12372-8{_}1935{_}*       *       *       *       *    Twice nine years are come and gone;   Karin the fair she walks her lone.
12372-8{_}1936{_}She sees around, on ilka side,   Maiden and mither, wife and bride.
12372-8{_}1937{_}Wan and pale her bonny brow,   Sunken and sad her eyelids now.
12372-8{_}1938{_}Slow her step, and heavy her breast,   And never an arm whereon to rest.
12372-8{_}1939{_}The old kirk-porch when Karin spied,   The postern-door was open wide.
12372-8{_}1940{_}"Wae's me!"
12372-8{_}1941{_}she said, "I'll enter in   And shrive me from my every sin."
12372-8{_}1942{_}'Twas silence all within the kirk;   The aisle was empty, chill, and mirk.
12372-8{_}1943{_}The chancel-rails were black and bare;   Nae priest, nae penitent was there.
12372-8{_}1944{_}Karin knelt, and her prayer she said;   But her heart within her was heavy and dead.
12372-8{_}1945{_}Her prayer fell back on the cold gray stone;   It would not rise to heaven alone.
12372-8{_}1946{_}Darker grew the darksome aisle,   Colder felt her heart the while.
12372-8{_}1947{_}"Wae's me!"
12372-8{_}1948{_}she cried, "what is my sin?
12372-8{_}1949{_}Never I wrongèd kith nor kin.
12372-8{_}1950{_}"But why do I start and quake wi' fear   Lest I a dreadful doom should hear?
12372-8{_}1951{_}"And what is this light that seems to fall   On the sixth command upon the wall?
12372-8{_}1952{_}"And who are these I see arise   And look on me wi' stony eyes?
12372-8{_}1953{_}"A shadowy troop, they flock sae fast   The kirk-yard may not hold the last.
12372-8{_}1954{_}"Young and old of ilk degree,   Bairns, and bairnies' bairns, I see.
12372-8{_}1955{_}"All I look on either way,   'Mother, mother!'
12372-8{_}1956{_}seem to say.
12372-8{_}1957{_}"'We are souls that might have been,   But for your vanity and sin.
